Go into the book with an open mindset, the way you should go into all books, and you should like it. It's painful to accept at first, but in the end you will lament that it is an accurate portrayal of the greedy, terrible human beings we have become. But do not be discouraged because to know despair, you must first know hope. Hope is abound in the book. There are people that you will find yourself rooting for to succeed, and the book benefits from this.<br /><br />The character development is among the most elaborate I have ever experienced. Over 100 pages into the book and I was still struggling to remember who was who. That's one of the greatest things about the book though. It feels so detailed that I almost felt close to some of the characters. It seems like they're all just a bunch of ants running around but it's okay that way -- it's how we are as humans.<br /><br />The book does not set out to be a feel good book. It's painfully realistic. However, the sheer number of problems, although realistic problems, seems unrealistic to be so abounding in a tiny town like Pagford. It's also perverse, but not unrealistically. Go read it! A book which has such mixed reviews means the only true judge of it will be yourself.

I absolutely LOVE Joaquin Phoenix and Philip Seymour Hoffman, and am also a fan of Amy Adams and Laura Dern and Rami Malek.  What could go wrong?<br /><br />Well, let me tell you...<br /><br />I was NOT a fan of this film, and it's a real shame.  While the acting was impeccable, the story line went nowhere, leaving me to wonder how a movie that ran so long could have so many holes in it.<br /><br />The opening thrilled me, even though there was much that had to be filled in by the astute viewer.  I didn't mind having to guess at the root of the problems facing anchorless ex-WWII sailor Freddie Quell (Phoenix)--there were enough clues throughout that I think a fair assessment can be made.  But it was how Phoenix displayed those trials through his body and face that floored me: his character is a man so uncomfortable in his own skin that he appears knotted. I will be shocked if he is not Oscar bound.<br /><br />As \\"The Master\\" of \\"The Cause\\" Hoffman, too, is at the top of his game, a charlatan who is so skilled with eloquent words and new age concepts that he carries himself like the cock of the walk, ebullient and charming and as smooth as the master salesman he is.  That his cockamamie \\"product\\" is as flimsy as gauze is beside the point: he tells his \\"patients\\" that he is helping them trace their souls through \\"trillions\\" of years of history...yes, TRILLIONS.<br /><br />That he--Lancaster Dodd--has a legion of supporters is not surprising; that he writes books, delivers lectures, travels, gains degrees, etc., etc., is not a mystery... snake oil salesmen would not exist without willing dupes eager to trade sane reason for quick cures.  What is fascinating is the interplay between Quell and  Dodd, both as a factor of the story and as two \\"masters\\" of their craft doing what they do best.<br /><br />So far, so good, as far as I have explained it.  Yet so much is left out or dropped from this tale that story lines are left hanging.  A minor example occurs in the opening scene in the movie: a shot in which a helmeted Quell peers timidly over a wall.  What that has to do with anything is absolutely unclear, and it is never developed why this navy man is seen as a helmeted soldier in the first place.<br /><br />Another, more telling one: the whole first two-thirds of the movie is wrapped around Quell's rampant alcoholism; yet at one point he is told to stop drinking and then the story line does nothing to detail whether he succeeds or fails.  That his behavior remains erratic is a non-clue: the movie has dwelled heavily on the details of his drinking, and then it is just dropped.<br /><br />There is a scene in which a furious Quell roughs up a person who questions The Master--what happened to that person, and to what extent the person was \\"dealt with\\" are left completely up to our imagination.  The same occurs again later in the film.  At one point there is a scene in which an important character sends signals that she is interested in an affair with Quell--what became of that, and why it was even included is a mystery.  The same goes for a scene in which Quell apparently imagines all of the women in the Cause naked.  Why is unclear.  One might argue that it is evidence of his intractable nature, but the scene is surprising and awkward and does nothing for storytelling.  Why it occurs there, at that time, seems ludicrous and smacks of sensationalism.<br /><br />There is a scene late in the movie in which Quell--who has run off from The Cause--is tracked down and is told that he must travel to England to see The Master.  Quell does, and appears shortly after in England (though the film does little to convince us that indeed this scene takes place there other than show some children in school uniforms).  Here  it takes only a moment for The Master and Quell to have their final scene, and without revealing too many details it is clear that all--The Master,  Quell, and the audience--have taken a long walk to nowhere.<br /><br />It is indeed a shame, for this movie began so promisingly.  That it had nothing to do with Scientology--which it was rumored to expose--is not the problem here.  What bothered me was that it was ultimately so empty...an exercise, full of effort, signifying nothing.ir final scene, and without revealing too many details it is clear that all--The Master,  Quell, and the audience--have taken a long walk to nowhere.<br /><br />It is indeed a shame, for this movie began so promisingly. 0824835921|4.0|No matter the route you take to learning Japanese you will end up learning kanji in one way or another. This book has you memorizing stories while supplying \\"particles\\" in which to reference figures you'll see repeatedly to make up a kanjis meaning. I'm surprised by how much it works honestly. Each \\"particle\\" is given a key word meaning which is referenced in the story to make you draw out the meaning of the symbol. 1451678193|4.0|This is a collection of Ray Bradbury's Mars colonization stories which were originally published in pulp magazines over a period of a few years. They are independent of each other in plot, but it is fascinating how Bradbury managed to pull them all together in a cohesive whole which told a story in itself. This book is considered the bridge between classic pulp science fiction (which targeted lowest-common-denominator audiences) and the more thoughtful and sophisticated modern science fiction. The stories have the same raw imagination as pulp, but each one tackles one or more social issues as well. The stories are fast and fun, and yet intriguing.<br /><br />My favorite story is about two missionaries bent on saving the Martians from sins that we humans haven't even imagined yet. The philosophical discussion of sin and the ironic use of Christian symbolism meshed surprisingly well with the sf-pulpy imagery. Bradbury also touched on evils-of-colonization, race-relations and xenophobia, and politics...to name but a few issues. I was also impressed by Bradbury's expectations of \\"the future\\" (1999 - 2020). Unavoidably, some of his themes were dated--we no longer worry about nuclear holocaust and (I hope!) lynch mobs are very rare in the US these days. He didn't foresee the civil rights movement or the cooling of the arms race. Despite this lack of foresight, he showed that humans never change. We may think we're living in an enlightened age, but xenophobia still exists and we're still willing to destroy the history and of an old land in order to set up our new world. Yes, I did feel that the stories tended to be a bit on the dreary side, but for some reason it didn't bother me so much because it was made palatable by Bradbury's fantastic imagination.<br /><br />This is a fantastic classic that any science fiction fan should read.

1400079276|4.0|Kafka on the shore follows two seemingly unrelated characters whose stories collide in surreality. The first character is a 15-year-old runaway boy who has renamed himself Kafka Tamura. Kafka runs away from his father for reasons that slowly reveal themselves as the plot thickens. He ends up in an obscure library, where he must overcome a dark curse. The second character is Nakata, an old man who suffered an injury as a child and lives as on a stipend for the mentally disabled. Nakata may not be very smart, but he can talk to cats, and he has an uncanny ability to accept surreal events at face value, thus providing a unique perspective to the strange plot twists. Kafka on the Shore highlighted the extreme effects alienation can have on a person's psyche. It had some VERY dark undercurrents (and even one scene of brutality that was quite shocking). It was a fascinating story, but after thinking about it for several days, I'm still unable to figure out quite what it meant. Perhaps it was only an expression of dark loneliness and nothing more? Whether I'm missing the deeper meaning or not, I greatly enjoyed reading my first Murakami book, and look forward to reading many more of these fascinating works.

B000021Y77|5.0|Tom Tykwer must've been hopped up on seven pots of Cuban coffee when he wrote the screenplay to RUN LOLA RUN. It's a nervy, jolting 1998 German thriller that hurtles like a runaway train. It's packed with urgent, kinetic moments. It's goaded by a pulsing techno score. It ambushes you with an unusual plot twist a third into it. It won the 1999 Sundance Film Festival Audience Award and plenty of other awards, so it can shout that from the rooftops.<br /><br />What ever happened to Franka Potente? She'd amassed so much cachet after her electrifying performance here, I'm surprised I haven't seen her more on-screen since. RUN LOLA RUN doesn't ef around. It right away establishes the two leads and the story's central conflict. Potente plays \\"Lola,\\" a redheaded whirling dervish of a girl who dashes off to rescue her frantic boyfriend Manni (Moritz Bleibtreu) who only has 20 minutes left to recover the 100,000 German marks that he'd lost, and then hand that over to his boss... his very testy mob boss. You wonder what an ordinary girl like Lola can do. I mean, besides run really fast and with arm-pumping abandon. One third thru the film, when things seem at its bleakest, maybe she'll surprise you. Because the thing that happens, I think she wills it into happening.<br /><br />***Hey now, SPOILERS alert for this paragraph!!*** Some say that this film is comprised of three parallel realities that showcase the same characters. Me, I prefer to regard RUN LOLA RUN as a time travel story, because it's cooler that way and it presents a stronger connective tissue. Sort of more [[ASIN:B000BZN1NM 12:01]] than, say, [[ASIN:6305210411 Sliding Doors]]. RUN LOLA RUN tweaks your senses with some metaphysical themes: free will versus determinism, pure chance versus conscious manipulation of events (see Lola's actions in the casino)... In each of Lola's run, she encounters various passers-by and demonstrates that even the most incidental contact can have far-reaching consequences. I really dig the flash-forward sequences as we learn, in quick-hitting snapshots, what befalls those passers-by, dependent on how their run-in with Lola went. Lola gets three do-overs, and each one presents a different outcome for one and all. That butterfly must be getting tired, flapping its wings so much.<br /><br />Artsy yet unpretentious, visually arresting and generating a frenetic energy, RUN LOLA RUN exhilarates. It makes me not resent reading foreign sub-titles. It makes me want to see more of Franka Potente, if only she would do more movies I had heard of. I mean, there's this one and then the Jason Bourne movies. cute. because what rabid capitalist would say, \\"yeah, i hate the earth. kill the earth\\" even as they're stockpiling carbon offset credits for a big, poisonous dump?<br /><br />it's focus. it's heart. it's what you'll live and die for at the end of the day. not what you wrote on your linked in or facebook resume.<br /><br />so who can disagree with the theory of feminism with an upper or lower case \\"f\\"?<br /><br />however, in PRACTICE, feminism has actually become about affluent white women having more of these beloved CHOICES: like regretting a half-dozen abortions during college, then adopting poor colored or third world children afterspending their reproductive years seeing how ambitious they can be, then having the inevitable epiphany that blind ambition's shallow and sucks, so then they can quit, stay at home, live on their husband's income, and write Atlantic Monthly articles about the guilt about giving up a good paying white collar job, while the rest of us are working three soul sucking jobs or clawing our way for just ONE.<br /><br />so they can be free to find themselves. choices like that are wonderful things, indeed.<br /><br />if we all had the luxury of exploring them.<br /><br />so what about the rest of us who're left to eat all this cheap high fructose \\"cake\\"? do we not have dreams? do we not bleed if cut and stabbed mercilessly? yes, we may have to sew our own stitches at home with the sewing kit, but we have dreams, too.<br /><br />i'm tired of working harder for affluent white folks to have more of that creamy \\"Justice\\" ones in the hospital when the rest of us were unable to even get ADMITTED into the hospital.<br /><br />it's all about class. and until we each have our own cab fare home, we're compromised, no matter 0345525558|5.0|This book is truly wonderful.  The writing is compelling, the characters are real and their lives move the reader - at different points my reaction varied from extreme frustration with and sadness for Victoria and her life situations, to joy in the beautiful and descriptive prose, to curiosity about the truth behind this language of flowers.<br /><br />The novel is told in alternating chapters - some from the present day, some from the year Victoria was ten years old.  That period in her life was her last childhood chance at having a family, before she became a permanent ward of the foster care system. Diffenbaugh paints a heartbreaking and vivid picture, not only of Victoria's past but also of her present.  Even as Victoria made me want to scream at her in frustration, I was riveted to the book in the desperate hope that a new page would bring her closer to  the love and happiness she seeks. Victoria's voice is at times hard, at times fearful, but always strong, as she relays the harsh realities of her time in foster care and reveals some scars that might never heal.  At the same time, I never felt preached-to by the author, nor did she ever try too hard to draw sympathy from the reader - rather, Victoria's complete lack of desire for empathy was the strongest draw for my emotions. I wanted to care more because she couldn't seem to care at all.<br /><br />I thought this book might be too 'chick-lit-y', I worried it might not be engaging. I was so wrong, I loved this book, I have recommended it to so many friends. 1601251556|4.0|Right off the bat, I have to agree with those who have pointed out that the Chessex vinyl battlemat is going to be more durable overall.  It's also easier to pack away, as it can be rolled and then folded without suffering any damage.  It is also much easier to get to lay flat after being rolled/folded.<br /><br />That said, I still prefer Paizo's basic mat.  Why?  Because it will take _any_ kind of marker, not just wet-erase.  How much this matters probably depends on your DM-ing style, but I like to draw out elaborate maps beforehand in permanent marker, then use wet-erase to mark anything temporary during the battle (zones and the like, or to keep track of positions if we have to quit in the middle).  I draw out maps enough in advance that I actually have five of these basic mats (and a few of their more specialized ones, but only ones that leave one side blank).<br /><br />The downside is that, once folded, the Paizo maps do not like to lay flat.  My solution was to find a large art carry bag that I could put them in _without_ folding.  If you don't need to transport the maps and have someplace that they can lay flat, that's even better.  If you do have to fold them, the best suggestion I've seen for getting them to lay flat is to put binder clips on the problematic creases, and to make sure to lay the map out flat a decent amount of time before you plan to use it.<br /><br />My take on it is that if you're a DM who mostly draws battlemaps on the spur of the moment, or doesn't mind the limitation to wet-erase markers, go with Chessex.  If you're like me and like to draw them out beforehand and want the markings to last a fair amount of time, go with Paizo.

B007KW55VK|5.0|By now, most everyone has heard the Karen Carpenter references in regards to Rumer's vocals. That may strike some as an easy comparison, but you can't escape the fact that Rumer really DOES sound like Karen on many songs. And for me, that's a most comforting sound. But Rumer is not a one-dimensional singer, and she is also capable of singing songs in other styles, too, as this fine new album demonstrates. \\"Boys Don't Cry\\" is a pleasing collection of covers of songs all written and originally performed by male artists in the 1970s. The album includes fairly well known tunes, such as Daryl Hall & John Oates' \\"Sara Smile\\" and Bob Marley's \\"Soul Rebel\\", balanced with more obscure compositions from big names such Leon Russell, Tim Hardin, Richie Havens, Isaac Hayes, and others. Rumer didn't select the \\"obvious hits\\" by these artists, but instead sought out album tracks that are less known. Clearly, she did her homework in picking songs that were not only suitable for her voice, but ones that really meant something to her. Hearing her sings these songs, the passion is evident. Perhaps the biggest surprise is the album's closing number, her cover of Neil Young's \\"A Man Needs a Maid.\\" That may strike many as an odd song for a woman to sing, but Rumer manages to put a new twist on those lyrics and transform the song into something less maudlin.<br /><br />Besides fine music, another plus to the CD edition of Boys Don't Cry is the accompanying booklet. In addition to the songwriting credits, each track listing has a cover photo of the album that the original song came from. Very cool touch! Hopefully, this might inspire a listener to seek out the original versions, songs by cool characters such as Townes Van Zandt, Terry Reid, and other fine singer-songwriters from that special era.<br /><br />I'm not bothered by the fact that this is a covers album. If Rumer needs more time to write some new original material, let her have it. No rush, as long as she is delivering quality music. And on this album, that's just what she gives us.sic. And on this album, that's just what she gives us.

It is a simply structured compendium of multiple snippets of 'research' --- the reporting of 'market' transactions whose outrageousness the author clearly will transport readers into a zone of transfigured moral superiority.<br /><br />The introduction reveals its game plan early: p8 'Why worry that we are moving toward a society in which everything is up for sale? For two reasons: one is about inequality; the other is about corruption'.  Obviously, Sandel is unfamiliar with Soviet Russia and Communist China -- whose income equality was extremely high, and where everything -- sex, cigarettes, toilet paper, legal approvals -- was for sale, and corruption the norm. Largely flat income distribution at bare subsistence levels didn't solve a 'moral' dilemma.  Yet uniform income distribution is an imperative of high academy-derived prescriptives on happiness.<br /><br />In fact, advanced capitalism merely offers a greater range of goods for exchange or barter.  It also -- thankfully for the author --- offers a wider opportunity for fallacious thinking masquerading as profundity and cloaked under institutional respectability. Sandel is evidently unacquainted with Kant whose statement 'From the crooked timber of humanity, nothing straight was ever hewn' might have given him pause before embarking on this presumptuous  pastiche in ersatz seriousness.  Sadly, far too many Harvard professors these days (not naming names) are indulging in this monetization of their professorial standing [sorry to have to diss my alma mater] - that in fact is the significant story here which merits a worthy research study.  Quite like Prof E Warren, the well-paid Harvard professor takes his or her $350K earnings, deplores income inequality, bashes other 1% earners and thus sanctifies her/her regular private bank deposits.<br /><br />When Sandel writes unequivocally on p14 that 'market reasoning also empties public life of moral argument', he bows in obeisance to one of his masters, Marx, if not the New Testament as well ('sooner will a camel...').  More importantly,however, unlike Marx, who was quite  sincere in his beliefs, this volume is itself a recursive pattern within the larger fractal geometry of the book as a whole: it is, in fact, an example of the 'moral limits of markets'.  This transaction is book for a publisher's advance and royalties...I hope the author seriously considers adding his recursive experience to the next edition, doubtless a cheaper paperback version. Is Sandel refused to accept a 'market' salary from Harvard, the privilege of the tenure system, or perhaps he has negotiated a below-market advance and royalty contract from FSG?<br /><br />Highly recommended to readers with strong confirmation bias who have strong beliefs on their moral (sic) superiority and would suggestively imply limiting the personal freedoms of others.ashes other 1% earners and thus sanctifies her/her regular private bank deposits.<br /><br />When Sandel writes unequivocally on p14 that 'market reasoning also empties public life of moral argument', he bows in obeisance to one of his masters, Marx, if not the New Testament as well ('sooner will a camel...'). 0143034901|4.0|Daniel Sempere is the son of an antiquarian bookstore owner in Barcelona, Spain. In the summer of 1945 Daniel's father takes him for the first time to an inconspicuous, but remarkable place called the Cemetery of Forgotten Books. It's to this sanctuary of the written word that books are brought in order to ensure they don't go from obscurity to oblivion. In the labyrinths of books being overseen there, Daniel chooses a book that he will protect himself: The Shadow of the Wind by Julian Carax. As Daniel reads Carax's book, he quickly falls in love with it and learns that it's quite valuable.<br /><br />For years, copies of it, and every other book written by Carax have been disappearing under unusual circumstances. One night Daniel is approached by a mysterious man who is horribly disfigured and who introduces himself as La&iacute;n Coubert; the same name the devil goes by in The Shadow of the Wind. Coubert offers to buy the book from Daniel and seems willing to pay any price for it, but Daniel refuses.<br /><br />That encounter marks the beginning of a life-long fascination with the tragic life of Julian Carax. As he tries to uncover the events that led up to Carax's murder, layer upon layer of mysterious circumstances are revealed and Daniel finds himself the center of much unwanted attention.<br /><br />The book is wonderful. Zaf&oacute;n mixes in elements from numerous genres: mystery, fantasy, romance, and horror, and he does so flawlessly. The writing itself is fantastic and the plot is complex and intelligent. It is the first of three books written so far involving Daniel and the Cemetery of Forgotten Books and I'm looking forward to reading those as well. The Shadow of the Wind is an international best seller and has been translated into over 40 different languages, fortunately one of them is English.

1455502758|5.0|I admit, I first heard of Christopher Hitchens on the day that he died. My Twitter feed blew up with literary aficionados mourning the loss of the writer.<br /><br />Hichens wrote without barriers. If he thought it, he wrote it. According to Graydon Carter, a fellow \\"Vanity Fair\\" journalist who wrote the foreword to \\"Mortality,\\" \\"He wrote often--constantly, in fact, and right up to the end.\\"<br /><br />Hitchens died in 2011, a victim of cancer that began in his esophagus and explored t he rest of him like the Western territory. He described his initial tumor as a \\"blind, emotionless alien,\\" though noted soon after that it was a mistake to describe it as an actual living thing.<br /><br />Mortality is not an inspirational book. Hitchens did not write his essays to better the lives of others. They do that on their own, and not in the traditional sense. His final book is a posthumous collection of his views on death. Those who have read him at all will understand how grounded a realist he is, and those who haven't will appreciate his frankness.<br /><br />Hitchens was a devout athiest, and received a lot of backlash for his views. One offended reader wrote on an anti-Hitchens website about the correlation between Hitchens' fateful diagnosis and God's tendency to smite those who blaspheme him. This particular comment he dissects with incredible religious knowledge and wit only available to the man who has no belief in the religions he understands so well.<br /><br />But, whatever his personal opinions are, writers like this come few and far between. He will be missed, surely. After news of his death spread around the social media and physical worlds, his books, including the essay collection \\"God is Not Great,\\" and personal memoir \\"Hitch-22,\\" re-established their hold on the bestseller lists.<br /><br />Hitchens left behind a wife, three children, and millions of readers. His final moments are captured by his wife, Carol Blue, who writes the afterword.<br /><br />\\"My husband is an impossible act to follow,\\" she writes. \\"And yet, I must follow him. I have been forced to have the last word.\\"<br /><br />But, as Blue states later in her memory of him, writers like Hitchens do not die with their bodies. They remain on our bookshelves and in our hearts, and they always have the last word.sband is an impossible act to follow,\\" she writes. \\"And yet, I must follow him. Unlike previous Batman internal musing, this was the first time we \\"heard\\" what Bruce Wayne really thought about Clark Kent, himself, Harvey Dent and his war everlasting.  This is what distinguished TDKR from a average Batman story, and made it a classic. In addition, a Latin-English dictionary and something on Latin grammer would be beneficial to have close by in case you have questions. B007GCY8FS|4.0|I am surprised that so many reviewers rate this book as a \\"1\\" or a \\"2\\" star effort.  The first 2/3 of this book are among the best of the Reacher series, where Child shifts back and forth between Reacher's point of view, as a passenger in a kidnappers' car, and the investigation of the kidnapping as conducted by FBI and other law enforcement agencies.  I think the device works very well and ramps up both the mystery and the suspense.<br /><br />The weakness of the novel is in the last 1/3.  Once it becomes clear that both the passengers in the kidnappers' vehicle and the individuals involved in the investigation of the crime are not who they seem to be, Child seems to provide the easiest and least interesting of the possible resolutions to the mystery.  [Spoiler alert]  Is the \\"kidnapped\\" woman really one of the criminals operating under a false identity?  Is she a secret Federal or State agent?  Is she really an innocent bystander?  Is the \\"eyewitness\\" really part of the criminal conspiracy?  Are the criminals going to poison a water supply?  Is the FBI agent investigating the conspiracy really a criminal traitor? 1451696191|5.0|\\"The Perks of Being a Wallflower\\" has quickly become one of my favorite books due to interesting characters, relatability to the struggles of teen years and Stephen Chbosky's excellent writing style. The book deals with some of life's most difficult aspects such as suicide, depression, rape, drugs, and even homosexuality. And these things are exactly why the novel is so powerful and easy to relate to, woven into the story seamlessly and naturally by Chbosky.<br /><br />The story is about Charlie, an awkward teenager who is writing a series of letters to an anonymous \\"friend\\" about his experiences as a high school freshman. Charlie is quite intelligent for his age, with a keen ability to analyze the world around him, which convinces his English teacher Bill to continually assign books for him outside of the normal class curriculum. Their student/mentor relationship is definitely a focal point in the story and a great one. There is also Patrick and Sam, step siblings and seniors who take Charlie under their wing to teach him about life and love.<br /><br />In the novel, Charlie describes to his friend his growing love for Sam, who is beautiful, smart and kind. Charlie wants to pursue something romantic with Sam, but she doesn't see him in the same way, at least at first. Without revealing too much, let's just say that their feelings for one another prove to be more than mutual. One of the sweetest moments is when Sam gives Charlie his first kiss, wishing the first girl he kisses to be someone who loves him. Their relationship is complex and touching, and it grows throughout the book. Charlie begins as a naive kid who has a crush on an older student and later becomes someone who understands and respects the love that Sam gives him, something rarely seen among people but is possible.<br /><br />Patrick was one of my favorite characters due to his loyalty to friendship and his secret lover Brad. In regards to friendship, he treats his friends well, especially Charlie whois new to many ideas and experiences they go through. For instance, Patrick described his being gay to Charlie, even giving intimate details about who was the \\"dominant\\" one in his and Brad's relationship. And with time, Charlie speaks of being gay as normal and not a big deal, something definitely needed in a diverse world.<br /><br />\\"The Perks of Being a Wallflower\\" is simply an amazing piece of literature. The characters are well developed and three-dimensional, and the issues present in the book ring incredibly true for many teenagers and even slightly older folks who recall their adolescence with nostalgia and even regret. For me, this novel was sad and painful during many parts, partially because I went through similar experiences as some of the characters and could empathize easily. And it is Chbosky's brilliant, enthralling writing that will quickly draw the reader in and bring the characters to life.

Although Hoover may have been a hard guy to completely like or admire, it just seems the creators had some issues with their subject that show through a bit too obviously. Also, the way the film reverberates between public (Hoover's formation of the FBI, Communist subversives, kidnapping of the Lindbergh's baby, etc) and private life (his close relationship with his overbearing mother, awkwardness with most other women,workaholism,cross-dressing and apparent latent homosexuality, etc) gives it a patchy feeling. There is nothing, for example, on the prosecution of the Rosenbergs or the FBI's role during the Vietnam War years. Edgar\\" is smooth enough to follow, and Hoover's conflicting qualities--e.g., insistence on professional conduct and limitations of the Bureau's jurisdiction, yet, defiance of his own standards by becoming a sort of Rona Barrett of Washington--are often quite intriguing. B006IYFCIM|4.0|Everybody would benefit from reading this collection of experiments with human behavior.<br /><br />I think it would be fair to compare the book's accomplishments with its declared goals. The author states the book's primary goal early on when he writes, \\"..We need to first figure out what forces really cause people to cheat and then apply this improved understanding to curb dishonesty. That's exactly what this book is about.\\" He continues to state, \\"Once we more clearly understand the forces that really drive us, we discover that we are not helpless in the face of our human follies (dishonesty included), that we can restructure our environment, and that by doing so we can achieve better behaviors and outcomes.\\"<br /><br />I appreciate the author's research that describes and delineates what his experiments chose to observe. For example, in one series of experiments, people lied in order to get tokens that a few seconds later were exchanged into money. These subjects cheated to an extent that was almost twice the amount of people who lied in order to directly get money.<br /><br />Another series of experiments showed that when people were tired or stressed, they were more likely to cheat. The amount that mentally exhausted subjects cheated was almost three times the amount of non-stressed subjects.<br /><br />There was also a series of experiments that tested the \\"what the hell\\" effect. The researchers found that the amount of cheating was not linear. A point was frequently reached where the subjects apparently decided to abandon a little bit of cheating, and started to cheat at every opportunity.<br /><br />The author proposes that a \\"fake it until you make it\\" approach doesn't ultimately lead to honest behavior. He suggests that it would probably start a chain of events that proceed through the \\"what the hell\\" context, where a little bit of cheating becomes a lot, and ends up with suffering when the truth is eventually revealed.<br /><br />But what these experiments examined, in my view, was not the origins of dishonest behavior, but rather the middle and ending parts of dishonest behaviors. As such, I don't see how the book's primary goal can be achieved.<br /><br />Without exploring the precedents to dishonest behavior, we are also left with a patchwork approach to achieving the secondary goal of changing outcomes by influencing the salient aspects of behavior.<br /><br />Understanding that I'm not an expert or a researcher, let me offer an approach that could be more conducive to achieving the primary and secondary goals of the book. In my opinion, the necessary but unexplored research area would be along the lines of, \\"What do I feel just before I act dishonestly?\\"<br /><br />I feel that the subjects' probable answers to this unasked question would indicate that the person's unfulfilled needs were in play. These needs are for the most part unconscious, and are the sources of automatic behavior that seeks to fulfill these needs. The outward manifestations of this automatic behavior will lead the subjects to symbolic fulfillment of their old needs.<br /><br />The subjects in the experiments may not be able to make the connection between their behaviors of say, cheating on a pledge to quit smoking, and their driving forces. This is probably because the subjects aren't consciously aware of the feelings they had just before they acted.<br /><br />The researchers may be able to bridge this gap with information obtained from measurements done by fMRIs and other instruments. They can integrate these measurements with the subjects' reports of their feelings.<br /><br />In my view, it is important that the researchers uncover the subjects' underlying feelings to meet the goals of the book. This is necessary because their feelings are closer to the causes of behavior, in my view.<br /><br />The subjects' behaviors are symptoms of their problems, not the problems themselves. The researchers would be better served to study the entiresituation as best they can.<br /><br />All of us anticipate while we read a book that there will be prescriptions and answers to the circumstances and troubles presented. But because The (Honest) Truth About Dishonesty doesn't identify \\"what forces really cause people to cheat\\", the primary goal, to \\"clearly understand the forces that really drive us\\", isn't attained.<br /><br />Realization of the secondary goal is undecided. The author presented several examples of how environments affect people's dishonesty, such as conflicts of interests. He showed how people's rationalizations allow them to permit a level of dishonesty that doesn't harm their ideas about their own morality. But how can effective and enduring solutions arise \\"so we can achieve better behaviors and outcomes\\" when the roots of the behaviors aren't examined?

0316098515|4.0|I will come out and say straight up that Eleven Madison Park cookbook is one of my all-time favorite cookbooks. The book itself is laid-out in an artful but logical and easy-to-read format. Special mention should go to the \\"back-of-the-book\\" recipes, which are organized into sections such as [fluid] gels, purees, ice creams, jus, butter, etc. I have never seen a high-end restaurant cookbook with such a vast section of component recipes. It goes without saying that the food photography is world-class and I have yet to see any cookbook with better photography.<br /><br />The recipes are obviously the heart of the book, and they will not dissapoint. This book features a collection of complex dishes, organized by season. Each dish generally features one or two star ingredients, along with a multitude of component recipes that serve to highlight that ingredient in very unique flavor combinations. For example, my favorite dish I've cooked so far out of this book was \\"Pork Belly with Mint, Peas, and Lettuce.\\" Other combinations are even more inventive, such as \\"Foi gras terrine with plums and bitter almond.\\"<br /><br />A word of warning: these recipes are not for the novice home cook. Many of the recipes call out for specialized equipment such as dehydrators, sous vide equipment, professional blenders, and liquid nitrogen. I do applaud the authors for attempting to make the recipes *slightly* more accessible to home cooks by offering alternative preparation techniques when available. Most of the sous vide recipes also offer an oven-roasted alternative preparation. Similarly, many of the liquid nitrogen recipes (such as \\"almond milk snow\\" and \\"green apple snow\\") also offer options to prepare using a standard freezer.<br /><br />I did take off one star for the use of volumetric measurements, which is a bit perplexing. It is pretty much the de-facto standard to use weight measurements in advanced cooking, but Humm has chosen to use volumetric measurements (cups) instead - which makes executing the recipes less precise. He still provides weight measurements (in grams) for some ingredients that require the extra precision, such as modernist ingredients (agar agar, xantham gum, etc). It is also worth mentioning that the recipes seem to be a bit salt-heavy across the board. I don't know if I'm just a bad cook, but I have more-or-less used half the amount of salt the recipes call for to achieve a reasonable saltiness.<br /><br />Make no mistake about it - these recipes will take a lot of time to prepare (I would estimate between 3 and 8 hours per recipe) and will often call for hard-to-source ingredients (unless you don't mind going online). As such, this book is probably only appropriate for adventurous home cooks and restaurateurs. But for those who are interested in this sort of thing, it's the best offering this side of the Modernist Cuisine.

But as the series went on, Giamatti and Linney disappeared and simply became John and Abigail.  The short, squat Adams seemed out of place in the pantheon of Founding Fathers.  This series shows that without his stubborn spirit (which was often not appreciated) the American Revolution would likely have failed.  And without Abigail's \\"ballast,\\" as John called it, John would likely not have been able to be the force he was.  In addition to seeing this country come into being, we see the intimate relationship of a remarkable couple as they face years of separation due to his work, tragedies with their children, scorn from the public and other elected officials, and difficult living conditions. Director Tom Hooper does not pretty things up, giving a deep sense of realism to the film.  What a great way to learn American history!

0312427999|5.0|The father of the Shock Doctrine is the late Milton Friedman, economist at the University of Chicago School of Economics beginning in the 1950s.  In his counterrevolution against the New Deal of economist John Maynard Keynes, Friedman foisted on the world his policies of privatization, free trade, and slashed social spending.  His first experiments were in Chile (where he consulted with Pinochet) and Argentina.  After laying waste to those two countries, Friedman and the \\"Chicago Boys\\" carried their destructive policies to Poland, South Africa, Russia, Sri Lanka, and other hapless countries.  More recently we have seen the disastrous results of his policies in Iraq, New Orleans, and the current financial crisis in the U.S.  Donald Rumsfeld was one of Friedman's adoring students.  Rumsfeld didn't pull the phrase \\"shock and awe\\" out of the air when attacking Iraq.  Friedman's theory is to either create disaster (Iraq War) or take advantage of disaster (9/11, tsunami, Katrina) and use the disorientation of the populace to put in place your new programs, namely corporate control.<br /><br />Journalist Klein chronicles decades of these disasters, country by country.  Likening free-market shock therapies to torture by electroshock, she demonstrates how free-market ideologues welcome, and provoke, the collapse of other people's economies. Her conclusion is a powerful populist indictment of the current economic orthodoxy.  In Klein's words:  \\". . the real track record of the free-market crusade: the dismal reality of inequality, corruption, and environmental degradation left behind when government after government embraced Friedman's advice, given to Pinochet all those years ago,that it was a mistake to try `to do good with other people's money'.\\"<br /><br />Klein documents that when some of the populace come out of the shock, become lucid, and begin to challenge the implementation of the policies (such as the loss of democracy, prices through the roof), the next step of shock doctrine is to terrorize,  torture, or to make the challengers disappear.<br /><br />Klein also tells about the International Monetary Fund, founded after World War II to help struggling countries and their economies recover. Many of its managers and policy-makers have been graduates of the Chicago School of Economics and have imposed the Friedman creed wherever possible.  Davison Budhoo, an IMF staffer, became a whistle-blower in 1988 and wrote a scathing letter of resignation.  He resigned, he said, after twelve years of \\". . hawking your bag of tricks.  To me resignation is a priceless liberation, for with it I have taken the first big step to that place where I may hope to wash my hands of what in my mind's eye is the blood of millions of poor and starving peoples.  . . Sometimes I feel that there is not enough soap in the whole world to cleanse me from the things that I did do in your name.\\"<br /><br />Klein says that the endless war with \\"Terrorism\\" that George W. Bush proposed was an expression of the Friedman doctrine.  It is not a war that can be won.  Winning is not the point.  The point is \\"to create `security' inside fortress states bolstered by endless low-level conflict outside their walls.\\"  She holds up Israel as the most advanced example of such a fortified, gated community.  \\"This is what a society looks like when it has lost its incentive for peace and is heavily invested in fighting and profiting from an endless and unwinnable War on Terror.  One part looks like Israel; the other part looks like Gaza.\\"<br /><br />Klein's summation of the Chicago School experiment:  \\"It has been one of mass corruption and corporatist collusion between security states and large corporations, from Chile's piranhas, to Argentina's crony privatizations, to Russia's oligarchs, to Enron's energy shell game, to Iraq's `free fraud zone.'  The point of shock therapy is to open up a window for enormous profits to be made very quickly -- not despite the lawlessness but precisely because of it.<br /><br />It is impossible to do Klein's book justice. B004J4WN0I|4.0|Drawn in by the promise of an exciting magic-filled adventure starring a modern-day druid, I eagerly snatched this story up when I came across it.  No disappointment here after reading it, either.  As always, I was taken with a new to me author creatively introducing me to his version of the modern world where everything of myth and legend really exists and lives side by side and amongst the humans.  It was a refreshing novel take on it because the hero wasn't a vampire, a werewolf or an angel, but a wise-cracking and witty druid who just wanted to be left alone to do his thing while instead he has a veritable queue of magical types lined up to make the last druid all dead.  And it was one exciting adventure after another to see how he craftily got his way through it.<br /><br />Atticus, though that's only the name he is stylizing himself as these days, is the owner of a specialty shop of all things natural and some things magical for the variety of residents of Tempe.  He has chosen to settle in Arizona for a very practical reason.  The more than two thousand year old druid is tired of living the vagabond life and just wants to be left in peace.  Arizona's locale is refreshingly devoid of ancient gods and the truly powerful nasties that walk the earth so Atticus shares his turf primarily with a vampire, a wolf pack, a witch coven and one minor mischievous local god, Coyote.  All this starts to change when he slips up and gets discovered.  Now an Irish fae god set on vengeance and acquisition has set his sights on Atticus forcing the druid to repel attack after attack while cunningly balancing traded favors with other fae gods and local magic users for help.  It was pretty exciting to see him in the role of the underdog hero.<br /><br />The plot was exciting, well paced and strong.  It was fun figuring out who was allied with whom and what they were all up to.  The story is told in first person point of view.  Slainte to Atticus who was a strong, engaging character with a fun, bantering 'voice' even when his back was up against the proverbial wall.  There is the refreshing addition of a great sidekick Irish wolf-hound named Oberon whom Atticus has taught to mindspeak and several other well-written minor characters that I really enjoyed meeting.  There is the mysterious Granuaile who is the bartender at Atticus' favorite Irish pub, Leif the Viking vampire who is his night-time lawyer and sparring partner, Hal the werewolf his daytime lawyer, his old Irish lady friend Mrs. MacDonagh who hates the British, and the bevy of freaky scary strong Irish fae goddesses who seem to either want to bed him or behead him.  Needless to say, I think the characters are the strength of this book to me.<br /><br />The main conflict of the story was resolved, but as is the beauty of Urban Fantasy there is the evidence of long range story arcs to enjoy as the series progresses with the blend of wit, danger and a little cunning.<br /><br />I caution those whose tastes lean toward the paranormal romance and heartily recommend this one to those who enjoy an Urban Fantasy that is strong on action and world building with a solid group of recurring characters led by a worthy character hero.<br /><br />Thank you to Net Galley for providing the book for review purposes.with a fun, bantering 'voice' even when his back was up against the proverbial wall. B007GCY8FS|4.0|Let's get something straight first: I am a great Jack Reacher fan, having devoured every Reacher novel and I agree with so many others that the selection of Tom Cruise to play Jack Reacher in the movie is quite frankly a commercial sell out and cynical mis-casting of monumental proportions. However, the terrible bad taste that it leaves in the mouth surely can't be allowed to bleed into reviews of A Wanted Man, as it appears to have done with a number of reviews I have read.<br /><br />Sure, A Wanted Man is different to many of the Reacher novels that have gone before. But it is still a great book! The action all comes at the end of the story, but I think the plot is interesting and well thought through, even if it perhaps slips away right at the end. The lack of action however I feel is replaced by a more thoughtful, even cerebral or reflective atmosphere, as Reacher sifts all of the incoming data and slowly works through the inconsistencies and red herrings to get to the truth.<br /><br />Another reviewer complained about the replays of the 911 call but I disagree - this again was evidence of the more meticulous, deductive approach that seems to be a constant thread running through the story. And some of this reflection seemed, for the first time in many Reacher novels, to be occasionally pointing a quizzical finger back at Reacher's own vagrant-esque lifestyle choices.<br /><br />And, without dropping a spoiler on any prospective reader, Lee Child still retains the ability to drop a massive unforeseen shock into the plot like an incoming RPG that knocks the reader backwards without warning.<br /><br />In summary? Can Tom Cruise wear Jack Reacher's shoes? No! Did Lee Child sell Reacher down the river with that decision? Yes! Is A Wanted Man different to previous Reacher novels? In some ways, yes. Would I recommend A Wanted Man? Definitely yes!!

0316228532|4.0|Note: This review contains no spoilers about the plot.<br /><br />For me, the most surprising thing about The Casual Vacancy is how ordinary it is. It's not a great book, by any means, but it's not bad either. It's a well-written, slightly cliched book that entertained me while I was reading it, but which will probably not linger long in my memory; it's the kind of book you buy, enjoy, and then leave gathering dust on the shelf.<br /><br />It's a very traditional novel in every sense. The plotting is decent, although J.K. Rowling has a tendency to use un-subtle signposts that let the reader know something important is about to happen. The characters are quirky, if unrealistic. The author's moral view of the world is a little simplistic, which suits the uncomplicated tone of the whole book. This isn't a book that'll challenge your beliefs.<br /><br />There were moments where I felt that Rowling was trying too hard to come up with memorable and effective descriptions. Some passages are a little too long, and the emotion of the narrative is a little too carefully constructed to be truly effective. There are one or two lines that were obviously supposed to be clever, but which stand out and distract from the flow of the prose. I was never quite as immersed in the story as I'd have liked to have been, which was a shame because at the core of the book there's a very interesting tale.<br /><br />So J.K. Rowling hasn't embarrassed herself at all with this competently written book. I felt at times like Rowling herself was a character, because I could sense her trying very hard to slip the Harry Potter style, which means (unfortunately) that some of the attempts to introduce sexual elements felt forced, as if the author was desperate to prove a point. I'd recommend The Casual Vacancy, but try not to set your expectations too high. It'll entertain you and, so long as you're forgiving of the occasional mis-step, I think you'll enjoy the book. But I doubt it'll ever top anyone'slists of favourites.

1451678193|5.0|Aside from hearing that The Martian Chronicles is a fabulous book, I had no expectations before diving in.  I knew it was a book about life on Mars, but didn't know much else.  I wasn't sure whether to expect Heinlen's Stranger in a Strange Land or Burroughs's Princess of Mars.  What I found was very distinct from either of those.<br /><br />The book is structured in a series of short chapters, each of which felt like it could stand on its own as a distinct short story.  Each chapter (perhaps with the exception of some of the shortest ones) had their own fun and interesting sets of character & environment developments, plot twists and story arcs.  At the same time, they are all bound together by the passing of time from the first story to the last story and the consequences and effects of each story on the life and world of Mars.<br /><br />Unlike the other 'life on Mars' books I mentioned above, this book envisions a race of Martians living very much like Earthlings.  In fact, for the first little bit I thought I was reading about Earth inhabitants living on Mars.  Instead they were Martians but with some of the Earth habits and quirks you might stereotypically find in TV shows from the 50s and 60s.  As the story progressed, the Martians definitely became their own distinct race with their own huge differences in behavior, community, rules and expectations.  I really enjoyed the way Bradbury did this.  He made the Martians immediately relatable by giving them Earth-like behaviors and traits but then quickly made them unique and intriguing by expounding on the differences of their world and their race.<br /><br />Within a few pages, we find that Earth is about to make contact with Mars.  Again and again and again.<br /><br />The results are consistently humorous and intriguingly provocative.  The interactions between Earthlings and Martians is a fun and interesting commentary on the way we all interact and deal with the unknown.  I absolutely loved laughing at the ridiculous and over the top reactions and interactions while at the same time thinking about the truth of the behaviors and wondering why it is we do the things we do.<br /><br />It was slightly off-putting the way Bradbury seemed to ignore some of the scientific realities of Mars.  I acknowledge that this is a work of science fiction and that it was written in the first half of the 20th century, but some of the elements struck me as a little odd for the first few chapters (such as the Earthlings being able to breath on Mars, the abundance of life both in terms of humanoid creatures and in other animals).  The way some of the behaviors mixed with mid-20th century America, I sometimes felt disoriented by the lack of \\"true\\" Martian planetary realities.  Fortunately this was very easy to ignore once I really dug into the story.  And thanks to the fast pace of the storytelling combined with short chapters and a short overall book, I found myself completely immersed very quickly and thoroughly enjoying the tale without worrying about \\"reality.\\"<br /><br />As a whole, I absolutely loved this book.  The full stretch of the story was very engaging from the initial Earth-Mars contact to the final pages of the book.  I also love the way the book is structured into a series of shorter almost stand-alone stories.  I had quite a few favorites but I love that I can quickly and easily return to and reread or share these favorites without worrying too much about them being \\"out of context.\\"  I really loved the combination of silly humor, amazing sci-fi creativity and thoughtful social commentary. B008S80P38|4.0|Superliminal - 3/5<br />Didn't really like it at first, but it grew on me. Very sharp and edgy, and reminiscent of \\"A City in Florida\\" from 4x4=12.<br /><br />Channel 42 - 4/5<br />Danceable and catchy. The drop sounds like the Ghostbusters theme. Wolfgang Gartner is a valuable contributor here. If I could change one thing about this track, I would make the drop at 2:44 last longer and come in earlier. Otherwise great track though.<br /><br />The Veldt - 3.5/5<br />Ah, The Veldt. We've had a lot of time to listen to and digest this one, so by the time I heard it on the album, I was kind of tired of it. Still, it's a soft, catchy track with a good melody and so-so lyrics. The voice, courtesy of Chris James is nice though and is a welcome addition to what otherwise would probably be a forgettable song.<br /><br />Fn Pig - 3.5/5<br />This song begins with a lot of buildup, and gives off sort of an epic vibe. Then, at around 2:52, we hear a simple synth line, which acts as a sort of second drop. After that ends, we're left with a very barebones crunchy, dirty synth. The second half, while not exactly bad, doesn't sound like it belongs with the first half. It just sounds like it would fit better in a different context. Still an enjoyable song, but a bit of a mishmash.<br /><br />Professional Griefers - 3.5/5<br />This is another one that grew on me. When I first heard it, I wasn't totally sure how I felt about the loud, screaming, forceful vocals of Gerard Way, frontman for My Chemical Romance.  As per usual with deadmau5 songs that contain vocals, the lyrics are rather empty and don't really mean anything, but if you're listening to electronic music in the first place that shouldn't be an issue. I've heard some say that it would be better without vocals, but I tend to think that it would just be a boring loop. The vocals really do  give it character. Back to the song, it's pounding and punchy, and would probably be a good gateway track for punk rockers looking to get into electronic music.<br /><br />Maths - 2.5/5<br />This is one of the record's weak points. It starts out with a pretty boring synthline followed by an empty bassline for far too long. The track does get more lively, but it takes too long, and by then most people probably will have skipped over it. Reptition in music is not a bad thing, but the beat that a producer bases the entire song on should be compelling, and well, worth repeating. This one isn't.<br /><br />There might be coffee - 5/5<br />And followed by Maths, we have one of the record's high points. It starts off with a catchy synthline, and evolves into a trancey, beautiful, upbeat production. This is one that many people will be rewinding to hear again.<br /><br />Take care of the proper paperwork - 2/5<br />I'm not sure what Joel was going for here. The track starts out with a few ambient sounds, followed by an industrial-sounding train-like beat. From there, it just gets louder until getting into a steady drum groove. Then we get various experimentation from what I presume is extra clanky, metallic VSTs that Joel had lying around. Overall, it's pretty messy, and I wouldn't listen to it again.<br /><br />Closer - 5/5<br />This one is fantastic. It takes a while to pick up, but when it does, you'll know it was worth the wait. The two-minute intro is a riff based on Close Encounters of the Third Kind, hence the title \\"Closer\\". The main beat is a bit reminiscent of \\"Slip\\" from Random Album Title. It's very addictive, and a sure standout on the album.<br /><br />October - 5/5<br />This is my favorite track on the record. From the second it starts, you'll be hooked. It's just the right length too, I didn't feel like it needed to be longer, as I usually do with electronic music. Although basic, the synthline is extremely catchy. This will definitely be one to be played at nightclubs.<br /><br />Sleepless - 4.5/5<br />This is a very different track for mau5. It harkens the return of the Stephen Hawking voice,or the voice that was present on \\"Sometimes Things Get, Whatever\\" from Random Album Title. The drum beat sounds like an actual drum kit, which is very different for deadmau5. I also hear a bass guitar in there, or maybe that's my imagination. I'm a big fan of the experimentation here, and if it wasn't on this album, I'd have no idea deadmau5 made it.<br /><br />Failbait - 3/5<br />What we have here is a Cypress Hill (a rap group, for the unaquainted) song with minimal deadmau5 influences. It sounds like it belongs on one of their albums rather than one of deadmau5's. It's not a bad song, it just isn't very original and the hook is pretty bad. Being a fan of rap, I was able to enjoy this track, but it certainly won't be for everyone.<br /><br />Telemiscommunications - 4/5<br />For the third time on the record, we have a dramatic departure from what we consider to be typical mau5, and as was the case before, I wouldn't be able to identify this as a deadmau5 track if no one told me. That being said, it's pretty good song. Although I've heard of them before, I'm not familiar with the work of Imogen Heap, the group featured on this track, so I couldn't tell you if it sounds anything like their typical songs. I respect the experimental aspect of it, and I like Joel's decision to make it the album closer.<br /><br />Overall, >album title goes here< is a very solid record, bar a few songs. I enjoyed it greatly and think that if you liked deadmau5 coming in, you will certainly like this album. Even if you don't like electronic music at all, there's probably something on here that you'll be able to listen to. The first ten songs are pretty much classic deadmau5 with the last three being more experimental. It's a good record. Buy it.<br /><br />7.5/10

The thing I remember most and disliked the most about Babel was the fact that the entire movie seemed to be one long silent montage of boring events. B008D4AKYQ|5.0|Big Big Train has always been one of those bands, to me, that puts out good music but have always just kind of ended up in my \\"mediocre category\\". Not great, not bad, but not worth reviewing either.  Over the last few days I have compared \\"English Electric\\" with \\"The Difference Machine\\", \\"The Underfall Yard\\" (about their best offering to date, in my opinion) and \\"Gathering Speed\\" (which did not do a whole lot for me). Of the three mentioned I would have to say that \\"English Electric\\" would be the quintessential Big Big Train album. They have finally refined their sound and song composition to a point where the Prog world needs to listen up and pay attention. EE PT1 is quite simply a wonderful listen!<br /><br />They have a very pleasant sound and are very interesting to listen to. Nothing bombastic, over the top, or too symphonic.  \\"Progressive Archives\\" labels them as \\"Crossover Prog\\" and I would agree with that. Not really too eclectic and a bit too unique of a sound to label them as \\"Neo Prog\\" with maybe some slight leanings towards the Canterbury sound. There are 18 guest musicians/vocalists with 12 musicians guesting on 16 string and wind instruments. The list includes a couple of heavy hitters like Andy Tillison (The Tangent) and Martin Orford (IQ and Jadis)This adds very interesting elements to each song. While not all play on every song (that would be too much) each song uses various combinations of the instruments to enhance the music and add very interesting elements. Care seems to be taken with song length (most between 7 and 9 minutes) so as to allow time for the song to develop and evolve and not wear out their welcome for those who do not like 20 minute long marathons.<br /><br />Two of the shorter songs \\"Uncle Jack\\" and \\"Upton Heath\\" actually have prog folk undertones with the incorporation of more traditional instruments of folk/bluegrass guitar, fiddle and mandolin. But each song evolves into much more than that. David Longdon's vocals on each, soar. One cannot help but hear undertones of both early Peter Gabriel and Phil Collins, but David's vocals are much more pure with the ability to easily slide up to the higher registers. His vocals, particularly on \\"Upton Heath\\", are simply perfect. I can listen to both over and over again.<br /><br />I think this album has the unique quality that it may be listened to at various levels of volume to obtain a different experience. (Not unlike some of Marrilion's work.) English Electric is pleasant enough to listen at lower volumes, and let it encompass you; yet expansive enough to really send some chills down your spine when you crank it up!<br /><br />I am going to have to chide in with another 5 star review. Especially when compared to their other work. BBT has really hit a home run here! The very good news is that is just part one! We get part 2 next year. I am not going to vote it as the prog album of the year just yet but definitley in the running!. B008I3QZKU|5.0|This is a big record with a big sound. Think the 80's. (Hot Fuss is losely about a guy who kills a girl but says he is guilty, gets out of prison, enters the military, gets obsessed with another girl and kills her too in the end.  Day and Age is about the cycle of life, birth, death, etc.).<br /><br />What I find interesting about Battle Born is it isn't as much of a story within a story. Rather the songs are all different characters with a repeating them of being in a rough spot and thinking about the old days when a relationship was new, etc. How and why have things changed, how am I going to react to the changes and the tough times? Take the keys progression on that remix, and on Battle Born the band has mixed into a lot of the songs here that same keys progression, slightly modified in some of the cases, and put them in the songs. Some are obvious, sme not obvious, but if you pay attention you can pick up on it.<br /><br />I wouldn't tell anyone to not get this record, I think it is great. Is it Hot Fuss?  heck no, but I was prepared to not like Day and Age cause of all the synthesizers and lack of guitar. Now it is my favorite Killers record that I listen to often - a lot more than Hot Fuss. I am expecting the same of Battle Born.
